  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d0d0962e-4044-478d-869d-cc5234f710e0'
      });

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer11095454613267486518">In Washington, D.C., a homeless man dies in Commander Gray Pierce's arms, shot by an assassin's bullet. But the death leaves behind a greater mystery: a bloody coin found clutched in the dead man's hand, an ancient relic that can be traced back to the Greek Oracle of Delphi. As ruthless hunters search for the stolen artifact, Gray Pierce discovers that the coin is the key </span>
  <span id="freeText11095454613267486518" style="display:none">In Washington, D.C., a homeless man dies in Commander Gray Pierce's arms, shot by an assassin's bullet. But the death leaves behind a greater mystery: a bloody coin found clutched in the dead man's hand, an ancient relic that can be traced back to the Greek Oracle of Delphi. As ruthless hunters search for the stolen artifact, Gray Pierce discovers that the coin is the key to unlocking a plot that dates back to the Cold War and threatens the very foundation of humanity.<p>An international think tank of scientists known as the Jasons has discovered a way to bioengineer autistic children who show savant talents -- mathematical geniuses, statistical masterminds, brilliant conceptual artists -- into something far greater and far more "frightening," in hopes of creating a world prophet for the new millennium, one to be manipulated to create a new era of global peace... a peace on their own terms.</p><p>Halfway around the world, a man wakes up in a hospital bed with no memory of who he is, knowing only that he's a prisoner in a subterranean research facility. With the help of three unusual children, he makes his escape across a mountainous and radioactive countryside, pursued by savage hunters bred in the same laboratory. But his goal is not escape, nor even survival. In order to thwart a plot to wipe out a quarter of the world's population, he must sacrifice all, even the children who rescued him.</p><p>From ancient Greek temples to glittering mausoleums, from the slums of India to the toxic ruins of Russia, two men must race against time to solve a mystery that dates back to the first famous oracle of history -- the Greek Oracle of Delphi.</p><p>But one question remains: "Will the past be enough to save the future?"</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="11095454613267486518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26466184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17871437687350780632">I absolutely love the Sigma Force novels by James Rollins, and this one was no let down. He is everything Dan Brown wishes he could be. The stories are smartly paced, well researched, intriguing and funny and thrilling and full of heart. The characters are well crafted and we get so caught up in their personalities, even without boring side plots that reveal their relationships. This one picks up after a very intense cliffhanger, so if you're new to the series I wouldn't suggest starting with th</span>
  <span id="freeText17871437687350780632" style="display:none">I absolutely love the Sigma Force novels by James Rollins, and this one was no let down. He is everything Dan Brown wishes he could be. The stories are smartly paced, well researched, intriguing and funny and thrilling and full of heart. The characters are well crafted and we get so caught up in their personalities, even without boring side plots that reveal their relationships. This one picks up after a very intense cliffhanger, so if you're new to the series I wouldn't suggest starting with this one. The first is Map of Bones, and definitely a great intro. That being said, this book can certainly stand alone if you don't mind not knowing the intimate details of their pasts. These books are great fun, and this certainly lived up to the rest of them.</span>

<div id="review_754539204" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/754539204" />
    <a title="Tim The Enchanter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5654692-tim-the-enchanter"><img alt="Tim The Enchanter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386188040p2/5654692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/754539204?book_show_action=true">Nov 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim The Enchanter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim The Enchanter" href="/user/show/5654692-tim-the-enchanter">Tim The Enchanter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5654692-tim-the-enchanter?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5654692-tim-the-enchanter?shelf=my-ebooks">my-ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5654692-tim-the-enchanter?shelf=4-stars">4-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer754539204" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8885811890477664974"><b> 4 Stars to an Old Friend </b><br/><br/>When I picked up <i> The Last Oracle </i> as my next read, I didn't realize that it had been nearly two years since I read the previous book in the Sigma Series. I was quite surprised to see how far I had fallen behind. Absence, as they say, only made the heart grow fonder. Picking up a James Rollins novel is like sitting down with an old friend. The flow of the story and the likeable characters are comforting and familiar. While re-entering the world of Sigma Force, the Last </span>
  <span id="freeText8885811890477664974" style="display:none"><b> 4 Stars to an Old Friend </b><br /><br />When I picked up <i> The Last Oracle </i> as my next read, I didn't realize that it had been nearly two years since I read the previous book in the Sigma Series. I was quite surprised to see how far I had fallen behind. Absence, as they say, only made the heart grow fonder. Picking up a James Rollins novel is like sitting down with an old friend. The flow of the story and the likeable characters are comforting and familiar. While re-entering the world of Sigma Force, the Last Oracle does little to shake James Rollins from the top of the Action-Adventure Genre. <br /><br />The Last Oracle intertwines action with history and science. We receive a brief history in Gypsy or Roma people and their ancient connections. We are also treated to an exploration of the plasticity of the human brain, autism and the scientific connections to things that we may consider "mystical". As always, Rollins has done his research and integrated seamlessly much like Michael Crichton did.<br /><br />Personally, may favorite Rollins Novels are his stand alone novels. I have never come to love the Sigma force as much as his solo reads. That said, I did name my son Grayson. True story. On one level, this novel follows a similar formula to the others in the series. Generally, this bothers me and leads to the abandonment of series. In the case of this novel and the others in the series, Rollins does a masterful job of crafting an interesting story with an engaging supporting staff.<br /><br />If you have not read any of the other Sigma Force Novels, do not fret if this is the only one you have to read. This could be read as a stand alone novel. While reading the previous novel would provide context for one thread of the story, the a Sigma Force newbie could read this and enjoy it fully.<br /><br />Another excellent installment in the Sigma Force Series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8885811890477664974" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6997307516244604112">As the world turns in this series, I am riveted to the next adventure as Rollins continues to capture the characters where they have left off. Monk, one of the leading men of the Sigma Team was lost to us in the previous novel, The Judas Strain, and presumed dead. He is back! Yippie!! I love him. He is surrounded by 'gypsy' children who have been genetically altered and kept near Chernobyl where there is a vast amount of radiation, still active. A team of mad scientists have a major use for thes</span>
  <span id="freeText6997307516244604112" style="display:none">As the world turns in this series, I am riveted to the next adventure as Rollins continues to capture the characters where they have left off. Monk, one of the leading men of the Sigma Team was lost to us in the previous novel, The Judas Strain, and presumed dead. He is back! Yippie!! I love him. He is surrounded by 'gypsy' children who have been genetically altered and kept near Chernobyl where there is a vast amount of radiation, still active. A team of mad scientists have a major use for these autistic savant young ones and the chase is on when a few of the kids decide to enlist Monks help to escape and save the world from disaster. <br /><br />Just amazing how much talent is present in these Rollins novels. Always fast paced, intense, factual as well as fictional and riveting! I expect at some point this series might wane, fade or fall short, but so far, no, not in this one. <br /><br /> </span>

<div id="review_26352550" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26352550" />
    <a title="Candace Salima" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1253385-candace-salima"><img alt="Candace Salima" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442478747p2/1253385.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26352550?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Candace Salima" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Candace Salima" href="/user/show/1253385-candace-salima">Candace Salima</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone.</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26352550" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer995582732902091429">James Rollins is one of my absolute favorite of authors and I anxiously awaited the release of this book after turning the last page of The Judas Strain, which I reviewed on July 27, 2007. Rollins did not disappoint!<br/><br/>The Last Oracle picks up where The Judas Strain left off. From Rollins website I pulled this description, most of which is also the backliner:<br/><br/>"What if you could bio-engineer the next great world prophet: scientifically produce the next Buddha, the next Muhammad, or even the next Jesu</span>
  <span id="freeText995582732902091429" style="display:none">James Rollins is one of my absolute favorite of authors and I anxiously awaited the release of this book after turning the last page of The Judas Strain, which I reviewed on July 27, 2007. Rollins did not disappoint!<br /><br />The Last Oracle picks up where The Judas Strain left off. From Rollins website I pulled this description, most of which is also the backliner:<br /><br />"What if you could bio-engineer the next great world prophet: scientifically produce the next Buddha, the next Muhammad, or even the next Jesus? Would it mark the Second Coming or initiate a chain reaction with disastrous consequences?<br /><br />"A master at combining historical and religious intrigue with edge-of-your-seat adventure, New York Times bestselling author James Rollins brings back SIGMA Force to battle a group of rogue scientists who've unleashed a bioengineering project that could bring about the extinction of humankind.<br /><br />"In Washington D.C., a homeless man dies in Commander Gray Pierce's arms, shot by an assassin's bullet. But the death leaves behind a greater mystery: a bloody coin found clutched in the dead man's hand, an ancient relic that traces back to the Greek Oracle of Delphi. As ruthless hunters search for the stolen artifact, Gray Pierce discovers the coin is the key to unlocking a plot that dates back to the Cold War, one that threatens the very foundation of humanity.<br /><br />"For an international think-tank of scientists known as the JASONS has discovered a way to bioengineer autistic children who show savant talents--mathematical geniuses, statistical masterminds, brilliant conceptual artists--into something far greater and far more frightening--all in hopes of creating a world prophet for the new millennium, one to be manipulated to create a new era of global peace--a peace on their own terms.<br /><br />:While halfway around the world, a man wakes up in a hospital bed with no memory of who he is, only that he's a prisoner in a subterranean research facility. With the help of three unusual children, he makes his escape across a mountainous and radioactive countryside, pursued by savage hunters bred in the same laboratory. But his goal is not escape, nor even survival. In order to thwart a plot to wipe out a quarter of the world's population, he must sacrifice all, even the children who rescued him.<br /><br />"From ancient Greek temples to glittering mausoleums, from the slums of India to the toxic ruins of Russia, two men must race against time to solve a mystery that dates back to the first famous oracle of history--the Greek Oracle of Delphi."<br /><br />I love the way Rollins takes an incident in history and creates a fascinating, page-turner which you will not put down until you have turned that last page. Yeah, that's what I did. I started The Last Oracle while I was waiting in line to see Glenn Beck Live. I stopped only to watch the show, and it was well worth watching, then crawled into bed as soon as I got home and finished reading it.<br /><br />This man can write! He takes us deep into the mountains of Russia, not far from Chernobyl, tosses megalomaniac Russian determined to rule the world, his crazy mother, secret combinations and organizations, gypsies or Romani and the Greek Oracles at Delphi. Mix it all up with the SIGMA force and you've got a ride that will keep you breathless to the end.<br /><br />This book is a must read. I thoroughly and completely enjoyed it. And if you've never read James Rollins before then be prepared. Because you're going to head straight to the bookstore or Amazon.com to purchase all his other books. And believe me, you'll like them all. And they'll be a wonderful addition to your home library.<br /><br />Hardcover: 448 pages<br />Publisher: William Morrow (June 24, 2008)<br />Language: English<br />ISBN-10: 0061230944<br />ISBN-13: 978-0061230943<br /><br />Purchase <a href="http://www.amazon.com/dp/0061230944?tag=thebookfilmst-20&amp;camp=0&amp;creative=0&amp;linkCode=as1&amp;creativeASIN=0061230944&amp;adid=0F7HQEKNYCG6NYTWAVNB&amp;" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Oracle</a> here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="995582732902091429" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2683811146" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2683811146" />
    <a title="Melanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23102950-melanie"><img alt="Melanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445470019p2/23102950.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2683811146?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melanie" href="/user/show/23102950-melanie">Melanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780062018014" href="/book/show/9939134-the-last-oracle">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23102950-melanie?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23102950-melanie?shelf=own-print">own-print</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2683811146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18244114469994828863"><i>The Last Oracle</i> was an excellent addition to the <i>SIGMA Force</i> series with an engaging plot that wraps up a thread left hanging in <i>The Judas Strain</i>.<br/><br/>Outside of SIGMA headquarters, Commander Gray Pierce is caught in a firefight that leaves a man dead with an ancient coin clutched in his hand. After the coin leads SIGMA to a relic traced back to the Oracle of Delphi, the race is on to solve the artifact’s mysteries while keeping it out of the hands of the unrelenting killers on their trail. At the sa</span>
  <span id="freeText18244114469994828863" style="display:none"><i>The Last Oracle</i> was an excellent addition to the <i>SIGMA Force</i> series with an engaging plot that wraps up a thread left hanging in <i>The Judas Strain</i>.<br /><br />Outside of SIGMA headquarters, Commander Gray Pierce is caught in a firefight that leaves a man dead with an ancient coin clutched in his hand. After the coin leads SIGMA to a relic traced back to the Oracle of Delphi, the race is on to solve the artifact’s mysteries while keeping it out of the hands of the unrelenting killers on their trail. At the same time, half a world away, a man awakens with no memory of who he is accompanied by two extraordinary children who are in a mad dash to stop mankind’s destruction before it’s too late.<br /><br />Both plotlines in this book are action-packed with intriguing mysteries for the characters to solve. Ultimately I enjoyed both plotlines but for different reasons. The plot following Gray has more action and I felt like the stakes were higher which kept me invested. I also thought the pacing in Gray’s plot was better and the story moved forward at a good rate which kept things from stagnating. The plot with the man with amnesia however, I loved because of the characters involved. I won’t give spoilers relating to the man’s identity, but his appearance made me genuinely excited and I was interested to learn how he ended up where he did. When the two plots ultimately merge, it was fantastically done and I thought the author did a great job blending the two character groups together.<br /><br />The characters in this series continue to be one of the best aspects of the books. In this book, we got to see more of characters who previously had smaller roles in the series. I was particularly happy to get more of Kowalski as he’s an excellent character and the most relatable of the bunch. Kat continues to be a favorite as well and I enjoyed that she had a bigger role this time around. We are also introduced to some new characters in this installment and of those Marta was definitely my favorite.<br /><br />Overall <i>The Last Oracle</i> was a fast-paced, thrilling read and I really enjoyed it. I’m looking forward to reading the next book in the series, <i>The Doomsday Key</i>, soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18244114469994828863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

My delightful friends supported my reading habit, and never once wondered (to me, that is, I have no idea what they said amongst themselves!) why I was asking for Rollins's books.<br/><br/>I wanted to read them because they are very entertaining, have enough plot and character development to keep me distracted from being in a public mental health facility, and are intensely visually told stories with deep sci-fi roots and some supernatural almost-Lo</span>
  <span id="freeText13712361466103175630" style="display:none">I read this while I was in the goofy garage during 2014. My delightful friends supported my reading habit, and never once wondered (to me, that is, I have no idea what they said amongst themselves!) why I was asking for Rollins's books.<br /><br />I wanted to read them because they are very entertaining, have enough plot and character development to keep me distracted from being in a public mental health facility, and are intensely visually told stories with deep sci-fi roots and some supernatural almost-Lovecraftian cosmic horror overtones.<br /><br />And this entry in the series was a real pulse-pounder! Goddesses below us, was it a ride, but we went some serious weird places. Unlike the others, I don't think I'd ever re-read this book. The sheer implausibility of the supernatural elements...well...waaayyy over my top. And the soap-operatic elements with the team were ratcheted up in this one, too, so there was a touch too much for my fullest enjoyment.<br /><br />Suited me down to the ground at a time when anything more or less challenging would've been impossible to handle.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2298985246898353543"><b>5 STARS (OR SOMETHING VERY CLOSE)</b><br/><br/>The Last Oracle, more than any of the previous books in this series, is a non-stop rollercoaster ride. There was no time to take a break, have a coffee or even go out to walk the dogs (don't worry, they did get taken out - just not by me) while I was reading this.   <span id="freeText2298985246898353543" style="display:none"><b>5 STARS (OR SOMETHING VERY CLOSE)</b><br /><br />The Last Oracle, more than any of the previous books in this series, is a non-stop rollercoaster ride. There was no time to take a break, have a coffee or even go out to walk the dogs (don't worry, they did get taken out - just not by me) while I was reading this. It was 48 hours of intensive page-flipping with few breaks. <br /><br />It starts with a single death and ends with many more. Rollins doesn't pull punches with his novels - he takes us close to the darkest depths of humanity and some of the atrocities we as a species are capable of committing. And if you read the Author's Note at the back, a lot of the experiments Rollins writes about have in fact taken place at some point in our shared history. A scary thought.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_41955491" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41955491" />
    <a title="Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1709422-jean"><img alt="Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1416431345p2/1709422.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41955491?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jean" href="/user/show/1709422-jean">Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1709422-jean?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer41955491&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41955491" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8881893675275088963">Sigma Force comes up against an unlikely combination of mysteries in this novel: autism and nuclear radiation. I found the plot of <i>Last Oracle</i> to be less tightly-constructed than previous Rollins novels. It took me a long time to get into it because it took so long for the plot to take any sort of shape. The characters were aimlessly following the clues given by a dead man and an autistic child for over half the book, with no clue as to what their goal was. <br/><br/>Oddly enough, I found all the characte</span>
  <span id="freeText8881893675275088963" style="display:none">Sigma Force comes up against an unlikely combination of mysteries in this novel: autism and nuclear radiation. I found the plot of <i>Last Oracle</i> to be less tightly-constructed than previous Rollins novels. It took me a long time to get into it because it took so long for the plot to take any sort of shape. The characters were aimlessly following the clues given by a dead man and an autistic child for over half the book, with no clue as to what their goal was. <br /><br />Oddly enough, I found all the characters (except perhaps Kat, Monk, &amp; Kowalski) to be flatter than ever, even though I "knew" them from previous novels. Most of the new &amp; secondary characters seemed either too over-the-top (especially Mapplethorpe) or too flat (Elizabeth and Rosauro) for me to care one way or another about their fates. The diabolically evil plot was a bit strange; it didn't make a whole lot of sense to me. There were too many holes for things to go wrong, to little connection between what they were doing and what they were trying to acheive (did they really think they'd come to rule the world by spreading chaos? Yeah, maybe, but there are too many variables on how that would turn out), and very little explanation of how the flashbacks to the 1950s related to their actions of the 1990s (I understand how they came to use the children eventually, but why did they steal them in the first place if they didn't come up with their current evil plan until so much later?)<br /><br />The best part of the book was the Russian Bad Guys, though. They had more dimension than any other characters in that they were so committed to both the plan and the children even though those concerns came into conflict. You couldn't help but adore Pyotr and Marta and Konstantin. (I don't really know why Rollins included the little epilogue about Pyotr/Marta. It was very emotional and special, but I didn't see the purpose it served, other than Rollins hates to kill off his favorite characters.)<br /><br />Speaking of killing off characters, we obviously get Monk back, big surprise there. It's a bit crazy how that happens, but that's the fun speculative part of the book: the harnessing of savant intuition and how far that power could stretch. OK. His relationship with the children was touching. He successfully came across as himself, just witout his memories. But I was disappointed that Rollins didnt give us any sort of reunion scene for us to witness Monk's initial reaction to finding out who he is. <br /><br />I could suspend my disbelief for the speculative aspects of the story; that's simply part of reading Rollins' fiction. But I have come to expect a plotline that's more engaging and makes a little more sense within that specualtive realm. And while his characters have never been the most compelling, usually they at least serve satisfactorily, unlike this time. Well, it would appear that we'll get some more of the Gray/Seichan storyline next time, which so far has been the most dynamic character relationship. (I just hope Gray perks up a bit and becomes more interesting.) </span>
  <a data-text-id="8881893675275088963" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9800252777244245163" style="display:none">Wow. Ok, I read this book because I saw a synopsis of it that said, "An international cabal is using autistic savants to bioengineer the next messiah." <br /><br />...wait, What!?<br /><br />Ok, I love the National Treasure movies -that is, I know they aren't good, per se, but I find them very enjoyable and entertaining. Last Oracle sounded like it had that same level of preposterousness, so I gave it a shot.<br /><br />Meh. Would I watch a movie of it? Yes. But that was almost the problem -at times it felt like it was just a simple description of what's happening to give to the CGI guys -rather than having a literary quality that would lend the impression of this being the finished text that your audience will be reading.<br /><br />Character development? Absent! <br /><br />And, I've been finding that most of the mystery/thrillers I've been trying to get into, despite if they are good or bad, are physically hard to put down. Well, this one was easy.</span>

  <span id="freeText6035676382879563200" style="display:none">This was the first James Rollins book I read and glad it was the first. The book hit a core in me as I read the story as it dealt with children, autistic or savant or just the fact they were children is unknown. It was tightly weaved with science and history which made it tie together. It was like watching a movie in slow motion but included thoughts and small details that might be missed on the big screen. It moved me like watching a good movie as I cried by page 410 and hadn't read a book in long while that moved me to such emotions. I am skeptical if any of Rollins other books would do the same but his style of storytelling is worth the time. I thoroughly enjoyed the book. The author's note at the end gave in to my suspicions of his historical data as realities and was glad he included that side note. </span>

<div id="review_82797740" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82797740" />
    <a title="Lewis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/696277-lewis"><img alt="Lewis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294367255p2/696277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/82797740?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lewis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lewis" href="/user/show/696277-lewis">Lewis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82797740" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14285779321811883586">Exploiting savant talents for military purposes is a fascinating yet horrifying prospect, which makes for a novel story. Kick up the intensity by making it exploiting the talents of autistic savant children who have been biologically altered to heighten their abilities, and you get Rollins' The Last Oracle. This fresh topic appealed to me from the very beginning and kept me turning pages quickly to see how things would turn out.<br/><br/>As you would expect with a Sigma novel, there is ample government se</span>
  <span id="freeText14285779321811883586" style="display:none">Exploiting savant talents for military purposes is a fascinating yet horrifying prospect, which makes for a novel story. Kick up the intensity by making it exploiting the talents of autistic savant children who have been biologically altered to heighten their abilities, and you get Rollins' The Last Oracle. This fresh topic appealed to me from the very beginning and kept me turning pages quickly to see how things would turn out.<br /><br />As you would expect with a Sigma novel, there is ample government secret agency involvement, espionage, and double crossing. The involvement of The Jasons was very intriguing. There is also a threat of radiation poisoning on a global scale. The reasoning behind the threat is even more interesting than the threat itself.<br /><br />While The Last Oracle is a book in the Sigma Force series, you won't need any knowledge of the preceding books to enjoy and understand all that's going on. Rollins does an excellent job of giving the reader all he/she needs to follow the characters and the story without ever getting bogged down. A perfect example of this is a character named Monk, who was in an accident and presumed to be dead in a previous novel, appears in this book. The mystery surrounding his accident is integral to this plot, but you need no prior info to follow it.<br /><br />The story offers much more complexity than having a single villain or even all the villains on one side or even in the same country. Fanaticism beyond reason is displayed on many sides. I enjoyed the varying levels of "bad" in the opposition in this book. While the story moves rapidly from one front to another (Washington D.C., India, Greece, Chernobyl), we do get to see the background and motivation that turns someone from being an ambitiously well-meaning patriot into a dangerous zealot, the road to believing that the ends justify the means. And, people do die, including some characters that you might not expect.<br /><br />WARNING spoilers ahead. Linking the mythology of The Oracle of Delphi in Greece through the "untouchables" at the bottom of the caste system in India to the fabled mystic abilities of the Roma (Gypsies) made for some interesting reading. The way the story is presented also provides genetic reasons for the special abilities; one needs not believe in any mysticism to buy into the story. This next part won't make any sense unless you've read the book, but I also thought it was a nice touch that the epsilon was included in the mythology of the Oracle which provided a link to Sigma itself.<br /><br />The ending is satisfying with just the right amount of closure and mystery that will continue into the next book.<br /><br />Some of my favorite lines and descriptions are:<br />"...Nicholas had seen piles of discarded gas masks, lying in limp heaps like the scalped faces of the dead."<br />"Roots tangled out of the sandstone walls, like the gnarled faces of old men..."<br />"...windows shattered into broken fangs..." </span>
  <a data-text-id="14285779321811883586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer19958609983388990">Another one bites the dust! Its crazy that I have polished off 9 James Rollins Sigma novels over a 7 month stretch. You would have never heard me say this during my run with horror reading but the adrenaline rush of reading stories that have factual baking is addictive. I understand that the stories can get a little far fetched but I am completely ok with it. Its almost like a guilty pleasure in a way. My question at this point as I am sure it is for so many others, how does he come of with his </span>
  <span id="freeText19958609983388990" style="display:none">Another one bites the dust! Its crazy that I have polished off 9 James Rollins Sigma novels over a 7 month stretch. You would have never heard me say this during my run with horror reading but the adrenaline rush of reading stories that have factual baking is addictive. I understand that the stories can get a little far fetched but I am completely ok with it. Its almost like a guilty pleasure in a way. My question at this point as I am sure it is for so many others, how does he come of with his content? How does he chose the best way to intertwine the history and the science? I am aware that writers do have their help but the individual interest has to be there for the rest of the story to flow and he gets it every time. This story in particular was a nice change from other stories as it dove into the fictional history of the Sigma program origin while taking you through quite the detailed history lesson regarding Chernobyl, which I'm pretty sure is a now a non-fiction interest along with the Smithsonian Museum. All thanks to the creative mind of James Rollins....I wonder when Seichan will make an appearance again.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18257535983844840361">The only other writers that makes me fly across the pages are Agatha Christie and Gail Carriger. James Rollins reminds me of my father, who instead of guided me in swimming, threw me into the water. His characters make no apologies about what they did and do, which makes his novels all the more gripping. <br/><br/>If we are to believe Rollins' world, then nothing in the history is truly what they were written down.   <span id="freeText18257535983844840361" style="display:none">The only other writers that makes me fly across the pages are Agatha Christie and Gail Carriger. James Rollins reminds me of my father, who instead of guided me in swimming, threw me into the water. His characters make no apologies about what they did and do, which makes his novels all the more gripping. <br /><br />If we are to believe Rollins' world, then nothing in the history is truly what they were written down. This, coupled with the author's countless references sometimes throw an incident a long time ago - incidents we thought were the dead ends of history - in a different light, evoking that imaginative notions of What Ifs. This time it's the sudden end of an Indian civilization that often was placed last in history book and the connection to the sibyls and future prophets. <br /><br />This is the reason why I love his books: the interconnectedness of seemingly disparate events and nations are unravelled under his skilled pen. </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2087956944" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17976765054727082735">A great adventure, yet missed opportunities within the larger Sigma Force context, The Last Oracle is frustrating at times. The concept of enhanced autistic children is very far fetched, though Rollins makes it work by humanizing the kids, allowing them to feel terror, love and even playfulness instead of being mini super people. There's a lot less chasing ancient clues around the world, however Rollins still lapses into formula, especially with the new scientist who is somehow able to kick butt</span>
  <span id="freeText17976765054727082735" style="display:none">A great adventure, yet missed opportunities within the larger Sigma Force context, The Last Oracle is frustrating at times. The concept of enhanced autistic children is very far fetched, though Rollins makes it work by humanizing the kids, allowing them to feel terror, love and even playfulness instead of being mini super people. There's a lot less chasing ancient clues around the world, however Rollins still lapses into formula, especially with the new scientist who is somehow able to kick butt with no training. The rescue of a key character is where The Last Oracle really falls short. A significant connection to The Judas Strain could have been made, especially considering the connection between a key antagonist and children. With how it's done here it just seems convenient. Overall, The Last Oracle is fun as always, though ultimately a little flat.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17976765054727082735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1717452249" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="emma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="emma" href="/user/show/23191346-emma">emma</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_701045664" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/701045664" />
    <a title="Janelle Dazzlepants" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1745684-janelle-dazzlepants"><img alt="Janelle Dazzlepants" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548040741p2/1745684.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/701045664?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janelle Dazzlepants" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janelle Dazzlepants" href="/user/show/1745684-janelle-dazzlepants">Janelle Dazzlepants</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1745684-janelle-dazzlepants?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1745684-janelle-dazzlepants?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1745684-janelle-dazzlepants?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1745684-janelle-dazzlepants?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1745684-janelle-dazzlepants?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer701045664&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer701045664" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6736889162303864316"><i>The Last Oracle</i> follows a similar path to <i>Black Order</i>, in that a Sigma operative ends up in the base of a nefarious foreign group, intent on killing the world through SCIENCE! In <i>Black Order</i> it was Painter with the Germans, in <i>The Last Oracle</i> it’s Monk with the Russians. The Germans were intent on creating quantum sickness with a device called “The Bell”, allowing their genetically modified offspring to reign superior. The Russians in this book are intent on creating widespread radiation sicknes</span>
  <span id="freeText6736889162303864316" style="display:none"><i>The Last Oracle</i> follows a similar path to <i>Black Order</i>, in that a Sigma operative ends up in the base of a nefarious foreign group, intent on killing the world through SCIENCE! In <i>Black Order</i> it was Painter with the Germans, in <i>The Last Oracle</i> it’s Monk with the Russians. The Germans were intent on creating quantum sickness with a device called “The Bell”, allowing their genetically modified offspring to reign superior. The Russians in this book are intent on creating widespread radiation sickness with the dismantling of the Chernobyl “sarcophagus”, allowing a single Russian leader to rise above with the help of autistic savant children.<br /><br />Go into <i>The Last Oracle</i> with an open mind about autistic savants. Simply accept that the differences in their sensory perception might allow for seemingly “superhuman” abilities. Simply accept that while they might have difficulties communicating and perceiving the world as we do, they may be capable of remote viewing, seeing into the future, or sensing heart and intent across long distances. If you’re capable of accepting all that then you’re going to have a good time! <br /><br />While all Sigma Force novels span more than one country, <i>The Last Oracle</i> is a little less linear than most. The story begins centuries ago in ancient Greece with the Delphi Oracle. Under fire from the encroaching Roman Army, the Delphi Oracle and a gifted child flee Greece to the area now known as Punjab, India. They assimilate into the local population but later flee into Europe to avoid persecution, where they’re now known as Romani gypsies. Unfortunately as history dictates the persecution doesn’t ease up in Europe. Russian soldiers hone in on a clan known for their mystical talents, massacring the adults and kidnapping the children. The children are then bred and experimented on over a series of decades, the Russians intent on identifying the genetic markers for autism and exploiting them to produce talented savants. <br /><br />While I previously compared <i>The Last Oracle</i> with <i>Black Order</i>, it shares some similarities with <i>The Judas Strain</i> too. Instead of using modern day science to explain seemingly miraculous acts in human history (like the m-state gold’s properties explaining away Bible events in <i>Map of Bones</i>), <i>The Judas Strain</i> and <i>The Last Oracle</i> follow historical trails to explain scienctific events happening here and now. <i>The Judas Strain</i> followed Marco Polo’s journey through Asia, where Sigma discovered that a naturally occurring and recurring cyanobacterial bloom in ancient Cambodia was responsible for a plague threatening to sweep the planet today. <i>The Last Oracle</i> follows the Delphi Oracle’s trail through Greece, India and Europe, to explain the heritage and mystical talents of the Romani gypsies and autistic savants. <br /><br />I love Greek mythology, but have read very little on the Delphi Oracle - surprising, given my predilection for all things weird and wonderful! I’ve also read very little on the Romani gypsies, though I’ve watched documentaries about the population in the UK and always found Hitler’s fascination with gypsies to be intriguing. And though Philip K Dick’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/697251.Martian_Time_Slip" title="Martian Time-Slip by Philip K. Dick" rel="nofollow noopener">Martian Time-Slip</a> presented a view on autism that I ruminated on for hours afterward, I’ve never read much more into it either! Half the reason I love this genre is the way it teaches me about history and science in a fun way, so it was great to see these three topics all woven together! <br /><br />While Sasha is the initial star of the story with her ability to foresee the future, Pyotr’s abilities are the real focal point of the novel. His ability to feel peoples’ “hearts” is curious enough, but Rollins continually hints at a dark secret between himself and Marta the chimpanzee. In the final showdown we learn that Marta and Pyotr are psychically and physiologically linked. Both empaths, they knew from the moment they met that they would die together. We also learn that Pyotr is not just an empath, he’s a conduit: he can draw on the power of other autistic savants, gaining their abilities if only for a short time. Unfortunately full use of this power comes at a massive cost, causing Pyotr’s consciousness to literally burn out. <br /><br /><b>Warning:</b> You <i>will</i> cry in this novel. It has an elderly female chimpanzee as a main character, so you know that means something bad <i>will</i> happen to her. If you don’t cry it’s scientifically proven that you do not have a soul. Seriously. I spent the entire novel preparing for an untimely death to come to Marta, and when it happened the floodgates opened and wouldn’t stop. I could barely see the words on my Kindle, I was crying that much over the visual of the elderly chimpanzee drowning in radioactive waste to save the children. (Note: I may or may not be tearing up while writing this….this is why I don’t watch Disney movies anymore!) <br /><br />Thankfully it was revealed that Marta hadn’t truly died, that her consciousness had been transferred to Pyotr’s shell of a body when his mind burned out. Not that that stopped the tears and tissues! I really hope that Rollins plans to bring back Marta/Pyotr in the future so we can see how she’s surviving. <br /><br />Speaking of surviving, OH MY GOD MONK IS BACK. Rollins managed to link the two novels together using Sasha, her psychic abilities spurring the Russians on to save him from death by squid (it sounds so ridiculous when you say it like that)! I wasn’t convinced that Rollins could let a newlywed father like Monk go so I was over the moon to see him back in this book. I’m hoping that the next book will see him take a safer desk job! <br /><br />I was also over the moon to see that Kowalski got a love interest! He’s no longer the thickheaded brute he was in <i>The Judas Strain</i>; we learn that he’s got a love of shoes, speaks Romani and actually has more than a dozen brain cells! I really hope the relationship with Elizabeth works out as it was adorable seeing their fumbling chemistry. <br /><br />It was a shame to see Sean McKnight go although I can’t say I was surprised. We don’t know much more about McKnight other than his history with Painter and his greying red beard. Rollins didn’t seem to care enough about the character to make the reader miss him, much like Painter’s non-descript aide that was killed off in a previous book. If anything, it’s a more a bummer to the reader because Sigma and DARPA will undergo yet <i>another</i> structural change. They remind me of the Russian and Chinese siblings in <i>Pacific Rim</i>: they were barely given 1 line so the viewer knew they weren’t going to survive the film. <br /><br />I wish that James Rollins had fleshed out the cruelty inflicted by Savina Martov at Chelyabinsk 88, turing her into a truly repulsive villain worthy of a Stephen King novel. Don’t get me wrong, I understand that she still retained some humanity in her soft spot for the children, but I think it would’ve contrasted better against tales of barbaric experiments. She was neither a fanatical zealot nor a true monster, she came across as a slightly nutty soccer mum that was a bit pissed about the state of Russia for the last 40 years. <br /><br /><b>Overall:</b> Romani gypsies, autistic savants, Greek oracles and nuclear meltdowns don’t sound like plot devices that should fit together, but they do. James Rollins solves the Romani lineage mystery, explores the potential of the autistic mind, gets Kowalski a date and saves our favourite character. Unfortunately the tale is a little fragmented and the villain not quite as “villainy” as we deserve, but it was a solid effort. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6736889162303864316" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2779821526" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2779821526" />
    <a title="OCHUNTER" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71228306-ochunter"><img alt="OCHUNTER" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2779821526?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="OCHUNTER" class="user" itemprop="url" name="OCHUNTER" href="/user/show/71228306-ochunter">OCHUNTER</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2779821526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10108550556308956439"> I rated this book a 3/5 but first to understand my rating you will need general knowledge of the story. "The Last Oracle" by James Rollins is apart of the Sigma Force series following a secret government organization with the main character always being Commander Grey. In this specific inclusion of the series is about the idea of bio-engineering the next prophet and what tragedy would come if done so. The book starts off with a homeless man being assassinated in Commander Grey's hands in Washin</span>
  <span id="freeText10108550556308956439" style="display:none"> I rated this book a 3/5 but first to understand my rating you will need general knowledge of the story. "The Last Oracle" by James Rollins is apart of the Sigma Force series following a secret government organization with the main character always being Commander Grey. In this specific inclusion of the series is about the idea of bio-engineering the next prophet and what tragedy would come if done so. The book starts off with a homeless man being assassinated in Commander Grey's hands in Washington DC. The old man gives him a ancient coin that the Sigma Force traces back to the greek oracle Delphi. This leads the group on a chase to save the world going to Greece, India, and Russia by solving the mystery. <br />James Rollins books usually follow such ridiculous plot lines and the plot isn't why I rated this book so lowly being that I wasn't expecting it to be really good. But this book stunk worse than the others in the Sigma series. My main issue with the book was how often it tried to bring up the moral and philosophical questions of artificially creating a religious icon. The reason why this peeved me so much is that frankly it wasn't interesting or really deep as it attempted to be. This detail ruined the fun stupid plot of random international conspiracies. Every time I was enjoying the filler action and thrill this thriller promised it was eventually ruined by the quote on quote deep question of artificial religious icons.<br />"The Last Oracle" wasn't a complete bust and had many enjoyable moments. The action sequences where truly enjoyable and made for a fun read. However, when ever morals was brought into the mix it just mucked up the momentum of the thrill. I would say that a good two thirds of the book I enjoyed. However one third of 428 pages is 143 pages and that can't be over looked. That one fatal flaw is why I rated the book a 3/5 stars. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10108550556308956439" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer552404949199187798">The story of Last Oracle is a combination of history of The Oracle of Delphi, The Origin of Gypsies, Autistic Savants & Chernobyl Power Plant. The Russians are harboring & manipulating a group of Autistic Savants, increasing their abilities with the help of scientists. The emotional connect with the children fails</span>
  <span id="freeText552404949199187798" style="display:none">The story of Last Oracle is a combination of history of The Oracle of Delphi, The Origin of Gypsies, Autistic Savants &amp; Chernobyl Power Plant. The Russians are harboring &amp; manipulating a group of Autistic Savants, increasing their abilities with the help of scientists. Sigma, An American secret military organization is set out to free the children. The persistent sway back &amp; forth of venues from here to there &amp; everywhere spoils the flow of the book. The emotional connect with the children fails to lift the level of the book. The plot is beyond belief and suffers from credibility as a result. A little too far over the top even for a fiction book, So I found myself losing enthusiasm for it. The novel was too far away from reality with arguable logic, hence falls short of expectations. SLACK &amp; DISMAL.</span>

  <span id="freeText16705905078157134372" style="display:none">Hmm...I really have to think about this review and why I wasn't crazy about this book. Maybe it's because I read it in spurts, trying to get into and comprehend and many characters and organizations they were from like the FBI, CIA, KGB, ABC, etc. <br />The story itself was good, with past-paced action, but even that got tiring after awhile-the protagonists saving the world just in the nick of time at the turn of every corner. I can't recall if I've read James Rollins before; his voice and style was easy to read. <br />Don't let me discourage you from reading this novel, others have rated it higher. To sum it up, I guess it just didn't float my boat. </span>

  <span id="freeText337370849992221212" style="display:none">Initially, I was very interested in the story line that continued from the previous book, but after a bit, I didn't care that much. What was nice about the first book was the complicated relationship between the main character and his father. There was no time for character development, because the characters never stopped being chased. All of these books follow the same formula. You know exactly what you are getting. This is book is very similar to all the others. I'll get back on board with Sigma Force and like it. I just need a little break.</span>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CuhriMVny9eXHk3cWyxKr+2OJZlqqdybRvkluXzpy3Q+pYOTupaIAsHgiDdak95wfFhZGVA5FJX0fbfX9TGtVA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zrPby6efdP0qWso6xxe6gAk4i2M4sRJfjjklVEvZMpj6/jPQ2G43KHykD9HGqC5fmO734wIh2lE8vbc6wgFUuA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

But fifteen hundred years ago, it vanished into the mists of myth and legend–its vast bounty of wisdom coveted ever since by scholars, fortune hunters, and those who believe its untold secrets hold the key to ultimate power.  Now a cartel of wealthy international moguls, bent on altering the course of history, is desperate to breach the library’s hallowed halls–and only Malone possesses the information they need to succeed. At stake is an explosive ancient document with the potential not only to change the destiny of the Middle East but to shake the world’s three major religions to their very foundations.   Pursued by a lethal mercenary, Malone crosses the globe in search of answers. Shortly after arriving, they find themselves involved with a cast of shadowy characters who all claim to share their quest. But as they learn more about the history of the treasure they seek, Rachel and Paul realize they\'re in way over their heads. Locked in a treacherous game with ruthless professional killers and embroiled in a treasure hunt of epic proportions, Rachel and Paul suddenly find themselves on a collisioncourse with the forces of power, evil, and history itself.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/38809.James_Rollins">About James Rollins</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/38809.James_Rollins"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1569545072p3/38809.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6104880462306503937">James Rollins is the #1 New York Times bestselling author of international thrillers. His writing has been translated into more than forty languages and has sold more than 20 million books. The New York Times says, “Rollins is what you might wind up with if you tossed Michael Crichton and Dan Brown into a particle accelerator together.” NPR calls his work, “Adventurous and enormously engrossing.” </span>
  <span id="freeText6104880462306503937" style="display:none">James Rollins is the #1 New York Times bestselling author of international thrillers. His writing has been translated into more than forty languages and has sold more than 20 million books. The New York Times says, “Rollins is what you might wind up with if you tossed Michael Crichton and Dan Brown into a particle accelerator together.” NPR calls his work, “Adventurous and enormously engrossing.” Rollins unveils unseen worlds, scientific breakthroughs, and historical secrets matched with stunning suspense. As a veterinarian, he had a practice in Sacramento for over a decade and still volunteers at local shelters. Nowadays, Rollins shares his home up in the Sierra Nevada Mountains with his two golden retrievers, Echo and Duncan. He also enjoys scuba diving, spelunking, kayaking, and hiking. Of course, he loves to travel and experience new places around the world, which often inspire his next globe-trotting adventure.</span>
